The Third Argument for Void and Its Initial Refutation
Razi presents and then refutes the third argument for void.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proponents' third argument for the existence of void and Razi's initial refutation based on the impossibility of preponderance without a preponderator.

Core Idea
If void were uniform, no body could move or rest without a cause, but a free agent can choose without cause – or can it?

The third argument for void claims that in a hypothetical void, a body could neither rest nor move because all locations are equal. Razi immediately presents a potential objection: a free agent could simply choose a location arbitrarily. However, Razi then refutes this by arguing that even for a free agent, choosing one equal over another without any distinguishing factor would be impossible. This lesson sets up the dialectic that the following answers will resolve.

Guided Path
1
Read the third proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage describing the third proof. Notice how the argument rests on the uniformity of void and the need for a preponderator for both rest and motion.
Translations are provided; follow the logical flow.
2
Understand the objection from free agency
The objection suggests that a free agent (al-fa'il al-mukhtar) can specify a body to a location without any cause, simply by intention. Identify this in the passage.
This is a common theological move.
3
Analyze Razi's counter-objection
Razi argues that if all locations are equal, the agent's power and will relate equally to all, so specification would still require a preponderator. See how he drives the objection back.
This sets up the need for the three answers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة لهم : لو فرضنا الخلاء، لكان ذلك الخلاء متشابه الأجزاء، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، امتنع أن يبقى الجسم فيه ساكنا ؛ لأن بقاءه في حيز معين مع مشابها لسائر الأحياز، ترجيح لأحد المتساويين على الآخر من غير مرجح. ويمتنع أيضا أن يبقى الجسم فيه متحركا؛ لأن الانتقال من أحد الحيزين إلى الحيز الآخر، تخصيص للمنتقل عنه بالهرب وللمنتقل إليه بالطلب. وذلك أيضا ترجيح لأحد المتساويين على الآخر من غير مرجح وهو محال.
لايقال: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن الفاعل المختار يخصص الجسم ببعض تلك الأحياز على التعيين لمجرد القصد والاختيار ؛ لأنا نقول : إذا كانت الأحياز متساوية من جميع الاعتبارات والوجوه، كانت نسبة القدرة والإرادة إلى كلها على السوية. فلو اقتضت القدرة والإرادة تخصيص ذلك الجوهر ببعض تلك الأحياز دون البعض من غير مرجح أصلا، كان ذلك ترجيحا لأحد المتساويين على الآخر من غير مرجح، وهو محال .
:
والجواب عن الأول: لا نسلم أن نفس الخلاء يقبل التقدير والمساواة والمفاوتة، بل الشيء الذي يمكن حصوله في الخلاء، تثبت له هذه الأحكام مثلا، ولا نقول الخلاء الذي بين طرفي الطاس أقل من الخلاء الذي بين طرفي البلد، بل نقول الأجسام التي يمكن حصولها بين طرفي الطاس أقل من الأجسام التي يمكن حصولها بين طرفي البلد.
والجواب عن الثاني: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن الحركة من حيث إنها حركة تستدعي قدرا من الزمان، وبسبب ما في المسافة من المعاوقة، تستدعي قدرًا آخر من الزمان؟

المسألة التاسعة والعشرون
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إذا فرضنا أن مائة ذراع من الخلاء، لا يمكن قطعها إلا في ساعة واحدة، ثم فرضنا ملاء أرق من الهواء، بحيث تكون نسبة ما فيها من المعاوقة إلى المعاوقة التي في الهواء نسبة العشر . فهذه الحركة تحصل في ساعة وعُشر ساعة، أما الساعة فبسبب أصل الحركة، وأما عشر الساعة فبسبب حصول هذه المعاوقة الضعيفة، وأما الحركة في الخلاء الصرف فإنها تحصل في ساعة واحدة فقط، فعلمنا أن ما ذكروه مغالطة.
والجواب عن الثالث : أن حاصل الكلام فيه يرجع إلى أن القادر المختار، هل أحد المثلين على الآخر لمجرد كونه قادرًا مختارا، أم لا؟ وعندنا أن يمكنه ترجيح ذلك غير ممتنع - على ما قررناه في مسألة القادر - قزال السؤال.
The third proof for them: If we assume void, then that void would be uniform in parts, and if that is the case, it would be impossible for a body to remain still in it because its remaining in a specific location while being similar to all other locations is a preponderance of one of equals over another without a preponderator. And it would also be impossible for a body to move in it because the transition from one location to another is a specification of the departed-from by leaving and the arrived-at by seeking. That too is a preponderance of one of equals over another without a preponderator, and it is impossible. It cannot be said: Why is it not permissible to say that the free agent specifies the body to some of those locations specifically merely by intention and choice? Because we say: If the locations are equal in all respects and aspects, then the relation of power and will to all of them is the same. So if power and will necessitate the specification of that substance to some of those locations without any preponderator at all, that would be a preponderance of one of equals over another without a preponderator, and it is impossible. The answer to the first: We do not grant that the void itself admits of estimation, equality, and inequality, but rather the thing that can occur in the void, these judgments are established for it. We do not say that the void between the two ends of a bowl is less than the void between the two ends of a city, but rather we say the bodies that can occur between the two ends of a bowl are less than the bodies that can occur between the two ends of a city. The answer to the second: Why is it not permissible to say that motion insofar as it is motion demands an amount of time, and due to the resistance in the distance, it demands another amount of time? The twenty-ninth issue. If you have understood this, then we say: If we assume that one hundred cubits of void cannot be traversed except in one hour, then we assume a medium rarer than air such that the ratio of the resistance in it to the resistance in air is one-tenth. So this motion occurs in one hour and one-tenth of an hour: the hour is due to the essence of motion, and the tenth of an hour is due to the occurrence of this weak resistance. As for motion in pure void, it occurs in only one hour. Thus we know that what they mentioned is a fallacy. The answer to the third: The gist of the discussion in it returns to whether the free agent can preponderate one of two similars over the other merely by being a free agent or not? According to us, he can preponderate that without it being impossible, based on what we established in the issue of the powerful agent. So the objection falls away.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refutation of the proponents' third argument for void. The proponents argue that if void existed, it would be uniform; therefore a body could neither remain still (since no reason to be in one spot) nor move (since no reason to pick a destination). Razi presents an objection from the opponent: a free agent (al-fa'il al-mukhtar) could simply choose to place the body in a specific location without cause. Razi then refutes this, saying that if all locations are equal, the agent's power and will are equally related to all, so specification would still be preponderance without a preponderator, which is impossible. Then Razi gives three answers. First, he denies that void itself has magnitude; rather the bodies that can occupy it have magnitude, so equality and inequality apply to bodies, not void. Second, he argues that motion requires a base amount of time due to the essence of motion, and additional time due to resistance; in void only the base time exists, so motion in pure void would take the base time—this refutes the claim that motion in void would be impossible for lack of time. Third, he addresses the free agent issue by stating that, based on his earlier discussion of the powerful agent (qadir), the free agent can indeed specify one of two equals without external cause, as a fundamental principle. Thus the objection is invalid.

Key Terms
خلاء
أحياز
ترجيح
مرجح
قادر مختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Razi denies the possibility of free choice or that he rejects the free agent's ability to specify without cause.
Correction: Razi does not deny free agency; he only argues that under the assumption of perfectly equal alternatives, even a free agent cannot arbitrarily choose without a preponderator. This is a logical point, not a theological denial.

Checkpoint
What is the core of Razi's counter-objection to the free agent objection?
A) The free agent does not exist.
B) The free agent's power and will relate equally to all equal spaces, so specification still requires a preponderator.
C) The spaces are not truly equal.
D) Motion and rest are natural, not voluntary.

Key Takeaway
Even if a free agent exists, acting in a perfectly uniform void would still require a reason to choose one location over another, making the void argument problematic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you choose between options that seem equally good, do you ever feel that your choice is arbitrary? How does Razi's argument challenge you to examine what truly 'preponderates' in your decisions? Will you commit to seeking deeper reasons behind your choices, rather than attributing them to mere whim?
?
If even a free agent cannot act without a preponderator in a uniform void, what does that say about your own accountability before God? Are there areas of your life where you claim 'no reason' for your actions, yet know that a reason exists? How might this truth drive you to align your choices with divine guidance?
?
Consider a time you believed you had full free will but later recognized unconscious influences. How does Razi's logical insight expose the need for a real ground in every decision? How can you cultivate a deeper consciousness of God as the ultimate preponderator in your life's choices?

Razi's Three Answers to the Void Argument
Razi gives three answers to the objection, each refuting a different aspect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's three detailed responses to the proponents' argument, including the nature of void, the role of resistance in motion, and the free agent's ability to choose without external cause.

Core Idea
Void is not a magnitude; motion has a base time; free agents can choose without cause.

After refuting the initial objection, Razi provides three constructive answers. The first denies that void itself has magnitude, transferring the properties to bodies that can occupy it. The second explains that motion inherently requires time, and resistance adds more time; in pure void, only the base time is needed. The third affirms that a free agent can indeed specify one equal over another by mere choice, referencing an earlier discussion. These answers collectively dismantle the third argument for void.

Guided Path
1
Read the first answer
Read how Razi denies that void has magnitude; instead, it is the bodies in void that have magnitude. Equality and inequality apply to bodies, not to void itself.
This undermines the premise of uniform void.
2
Read the second answer
Razi argues that motion has a base time from its essence, and resistance adds extra time. In void, only the base time exists, so motion is possible in a definite time. The example with a rarer medium clarifies this.
This refutes the claim that motion in void would be impossible.
3
Read the third answer
Razi states that the free agent can preponderate one similar over another without a preponderator, based on his earlier discussion. This directly answers the original objection.
This shows Razi does not reject free choice.
4
Synthesize the overall refutation
Consider how these three answers together defeat the third argument. The key is that the argument's assumptions about void and motion are flawed.

?

?

The Mathematical Refutation of Motion in Void
Ar-Razi presents a mathematical analogy to refute an objection about motion in void.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ar-Razi uses a mathematical analogy to refute the claim that motion in a void would be instantaneous.

Core Idea
Motion in void is not instantaneous; the opponents' argument is a fallacy.

In this passage, Ar-Razi addresses the third objection against the possibility of motion in a void. He constructs a mathematical analogy comparing motion in a hypothetical subtle medium to motion in pure void. This shows that the opponents' conclusion is mistaken.

Guided Path
1
Read the mathematical analogy
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Note the hypothetical: 100 cubits of void traversed in 1 hour, then a subtle medium with resistance 1/10 of air.
2
Understand the calculation
The motion in the subtle medium takes 1 hour (base) plus 1/10 hour (due to weak resistance). Pure void has no resistance, so it takes exactly the base 1 hour.
This contradicts the opponents' claim that motion in void would be instantaneous.
3
Derive the conclusion
Since the opponents' analogy fails, Ar-Razi declares their argument a fallacy. Recognize that the physical example is used to illustrate a logical point.
The refutation paves the way for the philosophical answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة التاسعة والعشرون
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إذا فرضنا أن مائة ذراع من الخلاء، لا يمكن قطعها إلا في ساعة واحدة، ثم فرضنا ملاء أرق من الهواء، بحيث تكون نسبة ما فيها من المعاوقة إلى المعاوقة التي في الهواء نسبة العشر . فهذه الحركة تحصل في ساعة وعُشر ساعة، أما الساعة فبسبب أصل الحركة، وأما عشر الساعة فبسبب حصول هذه المعاوقة الضعيفة، وأما الحركة في الخلاء الصرف فإنها تحصل في ساعة واحدة فقط، فعلمنا أن ما ذكروه مغالطة. والجواب عن الثالث : أن حاصل الكلام فيه يرجع إلى أن القادر المختار، هل أحد المثلين على الآخر لمحض كونه قادرًا مختارا، أم لا؟ وعندنا أن يمكنه ترجيح ذلك غير ممتنع - على ما قررناه في مسألة القادر - فزال السؤال.
The twenty-ninth issue. If you know this, we say: if we suppose that one hundred cubits of void cannot be traversed except in one hour, then we suppose a medium finer than air, such that the proportion of its resistance to the resistance in air is one-tenth. Then this motion occurs in one hour and one-tenth of an hour: the hour is due to the motion itself, and the tenth of an hour is due to the occurrence of this weak resistance. As for motion in pure void, it occurs in only one hour. Thus we know that what they mentioned is a fallacy. And the answer to the third [objection] is that the upshot of the discussion in it reduces to: does the free agent prefer one of two similars over the other merely because He is free and powerful, or not? And according to us, it is not impossible for Him to prefer that—as we established in the issue of the powerful agent—so the objection is removed.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is the completion of the 29th issue, specifically the answer to the third objection about motion in a void. The author first provides a mathematical argument: if 100 cubits of void require 1 hour, and a very subtle medium adds only 1/10 hour resistance, then pure void motion takes exactly 1 hour, showing the opponents' claim is fallacious. Then he addresses the third objection by reducing it to the question of whether a free agent can prefer one of two identical alternatives without a cause. The author affirms that according to the Ash'ari position established earlier, such preference is not impossible, so the objection dissolves.

Key Terms
خلاء
حركة
معاوقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument is a physical claim about the nature of motion.
Correction: It is a logical analogy to demonstrate that the opponents' reasoning fails; the physical specifics are hypothetical.

Checkpoint
What does Ar-Razi conclude from his analogy of motion in void and in a subtle medium?
A) Motion in void is impossible.
B) The opponents' argument is a fallacy.
C) Motion requires a medium.
D) The free agent cannot prefer one similar over another.

Key Takeaway
Ar-Razi uses a mathematical analogy to refute the claim that motion in void would be instantaneous, showing the opponents' reasoning is fallacious.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Razi uses a mathematical analogy to refute a false claim. In your own life, where might you be relying on faulty analogies or assumptions? Will you commit to identifying one such assumption and seeking correction?

The Free Agent’s Preference of Two Similars
Ar-Razi reduces the third objection to the issue of free agent preference and affirms its possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari principle that a free agent can prefer one of two identical alternatives without a cause.

Core Idea
The free agent's preference is not impossible; it is the basis of divine action.

After the mathematical refutation, Ar-Razi reduces the third objection to its core: can a free agent choose between two identical options without a reason? He affirms that according to Ash'ari theology, such preference is not only possible but is the very nature of a free agent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core question
Read the passage and find the question: 'does the free agent prefer one of two similars over the other merely because He is free and powerful?'
This is the heart of the third objection.
2
Understand the Ash'ari position
Ar-Razi states that according to us (Ash'aris), it is not impossible for the free agent to prefer without a cause. This is grounded in the earlier discussion of the powerful agent.
The 'free agent' here refers to God.
3
Recognize the resolution
Since the objection assumes that such preference is impossible, and the Ash'ari position affirms its possibility, the objection is removed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة التاسعة والعشرون
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إذا فرضنا أن مائة ذراع من الخلاء، لا يمكن قطعها إلا في ساعة واحدة، ثم فرضنا ملاء أرق من الهواء، بحيث تكون نسبة ما فيها من المعاوقة إلى المعاوقة التي في الهواء نسبة العشر . فهذه الحركة تحصل في ساعة وعُشر ساعة، أما الساعة فبسبب أصل الحركة، وأما عشر الساعة فبسبب حصول هذه المعاوقة الضعيفة، وأما الحركة في الخلاء الصرف فإنها تحصل في ساعة واحدة فقط، فعلمنا أن ما ذكروه مغالطة. والجواب عن الثالث : أن حاصل الكلام فيه يرجع إلى أن القادر المختار، هل أحد المثلين على الآخر لمحض كونه قادرًا مختارا، أم لا؟ وعندنا أن يمكنه ترجيح ذلك غير ممتنع - على ما قررناه في مسألة القادر - فزال السؤال.
The twenty-ninth issue. If you know this, we say: if we suppose that one hundred cubits of void cannot be traversed except in one hour, then we suppose a medium finer than air, such that the proportion of its resistance to the resistance in air is one-tenth. Then this motion occurs in one hour and one-tenth of an hour: the hour is due to the motion itself, and the tenth of an hour is due to the occurrence of this weak resistance. As for motion in pure void, it occurs in only one hour. Thus we know that what they mentioned is a fallacy. And the answer to the third [objection] is that the upshot of the discussion in it reduces to: does the free agent prefer one of two similars over the other merely because He is free and powerful, or not? And according to us, it is not impossible for Him to prefer that—as we established in the issue of the powerful agent—so the objection is removed.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is the completion of the 29th issue, specifically the answer to the third objection about motion in a void. The author first provides a mathematical argument: if 100 cubits of void require 1 hour, and a very subtle medium adds only 1/10 hour resistance, then pure void motion takes exactly 1 hour, showing the opponents' claim is fallacious. Then he addresses the third objection by reducing it to the question of whether a free agent can prefer one of two identical alternatives without a cause. The author affirms that according to the Ash'ari position established earlier, such preference is not impossible, so the objection dissolves.

Key Terms

مثلين
ترجيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's actions must have a reason or cause.
Correction: In Ash'ari theology, God's will is the ultimate reason; He can choose without external cause.

Checkpoint

B) It is possible.

Key Takeaway
The third objection is resolved by affirming that a free agent can choose between identical alternatives without a cause, as per Ash'ari doctrine.

The Philosophers' Proof That the Soul Cannot Be Annihilated
Razi presents and dissects the philosophers' first proof for soul's immortality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the philosophers' first argument for the soul's immortality and how Razi exposes its weaknesses.

Core Idea
The soul's simplicity breaks the chain of susceptibility.

This passage presents the cornerstone philosophical argument for the soul's indestructibility. The philosophers claim that if the soul could perish, its receptivity to non-existence would have to reside either in itself or in another; both options lead to contradiction. As you read, trace how Razi dissects each possibility and then addresses a potential objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense logical reasoning. Take your time.
2
Identify the two divisions
The passage states that if the soul were susceptible to non-existence, its susceptibility would be either inherent (قائمة بها) or in something else (بغيرها). Find these two cases in the text.
3
Understand the refutation of the first division
Why can't the soul's own existence be the receptacle for its non-existence? The text says that would require existence and non-existence to coexist. Think about why that's logically impossible.
This is the core of the argument.
4
Understand the refutation of the second division
If the susceptibility is in something else, that something must be matter-form composite. But the soul is simple; its matter would be simple too. If that matter also needs another matter, infinite regress follows. Trace this reasoning.
This leads to the conclusion that an ultimate simple substance exists.
5
Examine the objection and Razi's response
The objection claims the argument would make all forms and accidents indestructible. Razi's answer distinguishes forms/accidents (which depend on matter that subsists with them) from the soul (which is either immaterial or has a simple material substance).
This clarifies the scope of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الفلاسفة فقد احتجوا على قولهم بوجوه : الحجة الأولى : - وهي عمدتهم - أن الأرواح البشرية لا تقبل الفناء، وتقريرها أنها لو كانت قابلة للعدم لكانت قابليتها للعدم إما أن تكون قائمة بها أو بغيرها والقسمان باطلان، فبطل القول بكونها قابلة للعدم. وإنما قلنا بأنه يمتنع أن تكون هذه القابلية قائمة بها ؛ لأن كل ما كان قابلا بشيء فإن القابل يكون متقررًا مع المقبول، فلو كان الموصوف بقابلية عدمها، نفس وجودها لزم أن يتقرر وجودها مع عدمها، وذلك محال، وإنما قلنا: إنه يمتنع أن تكون هذه القابلية قائمة بغيرها؛ لأن مثل هذا الشيء يجب أن يكون صورة في مادة حتى يكون إمكان وجودها وإمكان عدمها قائما بتلك المادة فإن النفس إن كانت كذلك مركبة من مادة وصورة لكن النفس جوهر مجرد فمادتها يجب أن تكون جوهرا مجردًا، وحينئذ نقول : إن كانت تلك المادة أيضًا قابلة للعدم افتقرت إلى مادة أخرى، ولزم التسلسل، وهو محال. فلا بد من الانتهاء إلى مادة أخيرة لا مادة لها وتلك المادة لا تكون قابلة للعدم - وهي جوهر مجرد - فهي محل العلوم والإدراكات، فتكون النفس هي غير فتكون النفس باقية غير قابلة للعدم. فإن قيل : فيلزمكم أن لا يكون شيء من الصور والأعراض قابلا للعدم. قلنا : هذا غير لازم؛ لأن القابل لصحة حدوثها ولصحة عدمها موادها، وموادها متقررة مع ذلك الحدوث وذلك الحدوث وذلك العدم، بخلاف جوهر النفس، فإنها إن كانت بريئة عن المادة فقد ظهر الفرق، وإن كانت مادتها لا محالة جوهرًا مجردًا فتكون النفس هي هي لا غير، فتكون باقية.
As for the philosophers, they have argued for their view with several proofs. The first proof – which is their mainstay – is that human souls are not susceptible to annihilation. Its exposition is that if they were susceptible to non-existence, then their susceptibility to non-existence would either be inherent in them or in something else; both divisions are false, so the claim that they are susceptible to non-existence is false. We say that it is impossible for this susceptibility to be inherent in them because everything that is receptive by something, the receptacle subsists with the received. If that which is described as being susceptible to its own non-existence were its very existence, then its existence would have to subsist with its non-existence, which is absurd. And we say that it is impossible for this susceptibility to be in something else because such a thing must be a form in a matter so that the possibility of its existence and non-existence subsists in that matter. If the soul were composed of matter and form in that way, but the soul is a simple substance (jawhar mujarrad), then its matter must be a simple substance. Then we say: if that matter is also susceptible to non-existence, it would need another matter, leading to infinite regress, which is absurd. So we must end at a final matter that has no matter, and that matter is not susceptible to non-existence – and it is a simple substance – so it is the locus of intellects and perceptions, so the soul is itself, and thus the soul remains, not susceptible to non-existence. If it is said: this would entail that no form or accident is susceptible to non-existence. We say: this does not follow, because the receptacle for the possibility of their occurrence and non-occurrence is their matters, and their matters subsist with that occurrence and that non-existence, unlike the substance of the soul. For if the soul is free from matter, the difference is manifest; and if its matter is necessarily a simple substance, then the soul is itself and nothing else, so it remains.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is a definition and refutation of the philosophers' first proof for the immortality of the soul. Razi presents their argument: if the soul could cease to exist, its receptivity to non-existence must either be in itself or in another. Both are impossible. If in itself, then existence and non-existence would coexist. If in another, that other must be a composite of matter and form, but the soul is simple. If that other is also susceptible, infinite regress follows. Thus the soul must be an ultimate simple substance not susceptible to non-existence. An objection is raised: this would make all forms and accidents immune to non-existence. Razi answers: forms and accidents depend on matters which subsist with their non-existence, unlike the soul which is either immaterial or has a simple material substance, preserving the distinction.

Key Terms
أرواح بشرية (human souls)
قابلية للعدم (susceptibility to non-existence)
جوهر مجرد (simple substance)
مادة (matter)
صورة (form)
تسلسل (infinite regress)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' argument proves the soul is eternal in the sense of being independent of God's power.
Correction: Razi presents the argument as a philosophical proof that he goes on to refute; he does not endorse it as sound. The argument itself tries to show the soul is not susceptible to annihilation, but Razi's subsequent discussion challenges it.

Checkpoint
Why do the philosophers claim that the soul's susceptibility to non-existence cannot be in something else?
A) Because that something would have to be a simple substance like the soul
B) Because it would lead to an infinite regress of matters
C) Because the soul is composed of matter and form
D) Because the soul is a form in matter

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' first proof for the soul's immortality relies on the impossibility of the soul’s susceptibility to non-existence being either inherent or in another, but Razi shows both options are untenable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you knew with certainty that your soul is immortal and cannot be annihilated, what one worldly concern would you immediately stop worrying about? What would you start prioritizing instead?
?
The philosophers argue that the soul is simple and independent of matter. How does your daily attachment to material pleasures and physical comfort conflict with that truth? What is one tangible step you can take today to live more in light of your soul's permanence?
?
Razi presents this proof critically, dissecting it before refuting it. Are you willing to subject your own most cherished beliefs—especially about the afterlife—to the same level of honest scrutiny? What would you risk if you found they don't hold?

The Philosophers' Proof: Eternity of Time, Motion, and Bodies
Razi presents the philosophers' three-premise argument for the eternity of time, motion, and bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three-premise argument used by philosophers to argue for the eternity of the world, and the reasoning behind each premise.

Core Idea
The philosophers argue that if time is eternal, motion and bodies must also be eternal.

Razi presents the second philosophical proof for the eternity of the world. The argument proceeds through three logical premises: first, that time has no beginning or end; second, that motion is co-eternal with time; and third, that substances (bodies) are co-eternal with motion. Understanding this proof is essential for evaluating the later theological refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Razi is presenting an argument in three premises, each building on the previous.
2
Identify the first premise and its justification
The first premise claims time has no beginning or end. Underline the two parts: no beginning and no end, and note the reasoning based on contradictions.
The reasoning uses the idea that 'beforeness' is an existent attribute.
3
Trace the second premise
The second premise links time to motion: time requires change, so if time is eternal, motion is eternal. Identify the definition of time and motion used.
4
Understand the third premise
The third premise links motion to substances: motion requires substances that change, so eternal motion implies eternal substances.
This completes the argument for the eternity of bodies.
5
Reflect on the overall conclusion
The conclusion is that time, motion, and bodies are all eternal. Think about how each premise depends on the previous, forming a chain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية لهم : الابتداء والانتهاء على الزمان محال، فوجب أن تكون الحركة كذلك، فوجب أن يكون الجسم كذلك، فهذه مقدمات ثلاثة. المقدمة الأولى : قولنا : لا بداية للزمان ولا نهاية له، فنقول : أما قولنا يمتنع أن تكون للزمان بداية فذلك لأن كل ما لوجوده بداية فعدمه متقدم على وجوده، وذلك التقدم ليس نفس العدم ؛ لأن العدم قبل كالعدم بعد وليس القبل بعد، فالقبلية ليست نفس العدم، فهي صفة موجودة، فقد حصل قبل مبدأ الزمان شيء آخر متقدم عليه بالزمان، هذا خلف. فإذن لا بداية للزمان وأما أنه لا نهاية له فلأنا لو فرضنا له نهاية لكانت تلك النهاية ملحقة بالعدم، وذلك العدم متأخر عنه بالزمان، فقد حصل بعد أجزاء الزمان شيء آخر متأخر عنه بالزمان هذا خلف. فإذن يمتنع أن تكون للزمان بداية ونهاية. وأما المقدمة الثانية - وهي أنه لما كان الزمان بحيث يمتنع أن يكون له بداية ونهاية وجب أن تكون الحركة كذلك - فالدليل عليه أن الزمان عبارة عما ينقسم إلى الماضي وإلى المستقبل، فلو لم يكن هناك شيء يتغير من حالة إلى حالة لم يصدق أنه مضى أمر وسيجيء أمر آخر، فثبت أن الزمان لا يعقل حصوله إلا عند حصول التغير، ولا نعني بالحركة إلا هذا التغير فيلزم من قدم الزمان قدم الحركة. وأما المقدمة الثالثة : - وهي أنه متى كانت الحركة قديمة كانت الذوات قديمة - فهي ظاهرة ؛ لأن التغير والانتقال لا يعقل تحققه إلا عند وجود ذوات تعرض لها هذه التغيرات فيلزم من قدم الحركة قدم الذوات، فثبت أنه يلزم من هذه الشبهة القول يكون الزمان أبديًا أزليّا، وكون الحركة كذلك، وكون الذوات كذلك.
The second proof for them: beginning and ending for time is impossible, so motion must be likewise, so body must be likewise. These are three premises. The first premise: our statement: time has no beginning and no end. We say: as for our statement that it is impossible for time to have a beginning, that is because everything that has a beginning to its existence, its non-existence precedes its existence, and that precedence is not the non-existence itself; because non-existence before is like non-existence after, and the 'before' is not the 'after'. So 'before-ness' is not non-existence itself; it is an existent attribute. So there would be something prior to the beginning of time that precedes it in time – this is a contradiction. Therefore time has no beginning. As for it having no end, because if we suppose it has an end, that end would be attached to non-existence, and that non-existence would be posterior to it in time, so there would be something after the parts of time that is posterior to it in time – this is a contradiction. Therefore it is impossible for time to have a beginning or an end. As for the second premise – that since time is such that it is impossible for it to have a beginning and end, motion must be likewise – the proof for it is that time is that which is divided into past and future. If there were no thing that changes from one state to another, it would not be true that something has passed and another thing will come. So it is established that time is not intelligible except when change occurs, and by 'motion' we mean this change. So from the eternity of time follows the eternity of motion. As for the third premise – that whenever motion is eternal, substances (dhawat) are eternal – it is obvious, because change and transition are not intelligible except with the existence of substances that undergo these changes. So from the eternity of motion follows the eternity of substances. So it is established that from this argument follows the statement that time is eternal and everlasting, that motion is likewise, and that substances are likewise.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is a proof: the philosophers argue for the eternity of the world by three premises. First premise: time has no beginning or end. If time had a beginning, its prior non-existence would be a relation of 'before' that is an existent attribute, requiring something before time – contradiction. Similarly for an end. Second premise: time requires change (motion), so if time is eternal, motion is eternal. Third premise: motion requires substances, so substances are eternal. The conclusion: time, motion, and bodies are all eternal.

Key Terms
زمان (time)
حركة (motion)
ذوات (substances)
بداية (beginning)
نهاية (end)
تقدم (precedence)
تأخر (posteriority)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi endorses the philosophers' proof for the eternity of the world.
Correction: Razi presents this argument as part of his critique; he will later refute it from an Ash'ari perspective.

Checkpoint
According to the first premise, why is it impossible for time to have a beginning?
A) Because time is a substance and substances are eternal
B) Because the 'before' of time would require an existent attribute prior to time, leading to a contradiction
C) Because non-existence cannot precede existence
D) Because motion would then also have a beginning

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' second proof for the eternity of the world relies on a chain: time is eternal → motion is eternal → bodies are eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers argue that time cannot have a beginning because that would imply a 'before' time. Have you ever felt trapped by your own logic when trying to understand God's eternity? In what area of your life do you need to submit to revelation rather than your own reasoning?
?
If time is defined by change, then every moment of your life is a witness to change—your heartbeat, your thoughts, your repentance. Are you using your moments of change to draw closer to the One who is unchanging, or are you letting them pass without reflection?
?
This proof assumes motion and bodies are eternal, which contradicts the Islamic belief that God created all things. When you look at the world around you, do you see it as created and dependent, or do you sometimes act as if it is permanent? How can you remind yourself daily of its temporariness?

Direction and the Corporeal Determiner
Razi defines direction and asserts the need for a corporeal determiner.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why direction, being perceptible and finite, must be determined by a corporeal limit such as the surrounding sphere and center.

Core Idea
Direction points to a physical boundary.

The philosophers begin their third proof by clarifying what direction is: a perceptible goal of any moving object. Since direction is finite, it must be limited by something corporeal — namely, the surrounding sphere and the center. This lesson unpacks those premises before the main argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage defines direction (al-jiha) explicitly.
2
Identify what direction means
The text says direction is 'something perceptible by sense' and 'the goal of the moving object.' Understand that direction is sensed and exists.
This is a key premise: direction is real, not abstract.
3
Understand the implication of finitude
Since direction is finite (limited), it requires a corporeal determiner. Ask yourself: why must it be corporeal? The text implies that only a body can limit direction.
The determiner is said to be 'corporeal' (jismani).
4
Note the two determiners
The text states determination occurs only 'by the surrounding sphere and the center.' This sets up the identity of the determiner.
The surrounding sphere and center are the specific corporeal limits.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة لهم : الجهة شيء مشار إليه بالحس - وهو مقصد المتحرك - وكل ما كان كذلك فهو موجود وهذه الجهة محدودة، فلا بد لها من محدد جسماني، ولا يحصل هذا التحديد إلا بالمحيط والمركز، وتقرير هذه المقدمات قد ذكرت في الكتب الحكمية. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : هذا المحدد غير قابل للحركة المستقيمة ؛ لأن الحركة المستقيمة عبارة عن الانتقال من جهة إلى جهة وكل ما صح عليه ذلك كانت الجهة المنتقل عنها والجهة المنتقل إليها محددة قبل ذلك الجسم المنتقل إليها، فيلزم حصول الجهة قبل حصول علتها، وذلك ،محال، وإذا كان كذلك ثبت أن الحركة المستقيمة ممتنعة على الفلك المحدد، وإذا كان كذلك كان الخرق والالتئام ممتنعا عليه.
The third proof for them: direction is something perceptible by sense – and it is the goal of the moving object – and everything like that is existent. This direction is finite, so it must have a corporeal determiner, and this determination does not occur except by the surrounding sphere and the center. The exposition of these premises has been mentioned in the philosophical books. Once this is established, we say: this determiner is not susceptible to rectilinear motion, because rectilinear motion is a transition from one direction to another, and whenever something admits that, the direction from which it moves and the direction to which it moves would be determined before that moving body reaches them, thus the direction would exist before its cause, which is absurd. If that is so, it is established that rectilinear motion is impossible for the determined sphere, and if that is so, then perforation and fusion (kharq wa iltiam) are impossible for it.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is a proof: the philosophers argue that the sphere (falak) is limited and determined by the surrounding sphere and center. Since rectilinear motion would require directions to exist before the moving body reaches them, and those directions are themselves determined by the sphere, this leads to a circular causality. Therefore, the sphere cannot undergo rectilinear motion, and consequently cannot undergo generation and corruption (kharq wa iltiam), implying its eternity.

Key Terms
جهة (direction)
محدد جسماني (corporeal determiner)
محیط (surrounding)
مركز (center)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the determination of direction require?
A) A spiritual abstract concept
B) A corporeal determiner
C) The movement of the object
D) The sense perception alone

Key Takeaway
Direction, being perceptible and finite, must be determined by a corporeal boundary such as the surrounding sphere and center.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face the qibla in prayer, do you ever think of that direction as a finite, physical reality limited by the earth and sky? How does that awareness change the way you connect with the One who is beyond all direction?
?
If every finite direction points to a physical boundary, what does your own physical existence—your body, your time, your place—tell you about the boundaries you must acknowledge? Are you living as if you are unlimited, or do you humbly accept your finitude before the Creator?
?
The philosophers argue that direction requires a determiner. What determines the direction of your life? Is it a physical goal, a worldly pursuit, or something higher? In light of this lesson, what needs to change about the 'direction' you are moving in?

Why the Sphere Cannot Move Rectilinearly
Razi presents the argument that the sphere's rectilinear motion would entail a logical absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the circular causality argument that prevents the sphere from moving in a straight line, thereby ruling out generation and corruption.

Core Idea
Rectilinear motion would make the cause depend on the effect.

Having established that the sphere is a corporeal determiner, the philosophers now argue that such a determiner cannot undergo rectilinear motion. Their reasoning hinges on a causal priority: rectilinear motion would require the directions to exist before the moving body reaches them, which would make the direction exist before its cause — an absurdity. This lesson unpacks that logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice that this is the second part of the proof.
2
Understand the definition of rectilinear motion
The text defines it as 'a transition from one direction to another.' This is the starting point.
Rectilinear motion involves direction change.
3
Grasp the causal problem
If such motion occurs, the departure and arrival directions must be determined before the moving body reaches them. But those directions are determined by the sphere itself — causing a circularity.
This is the key absurdity: direction existing before its cause.
4
See the consequence
If rectilinear motion is impossible, then perforation and fusion (generation/corruption) are also impossible for the sphere.
This supports the philosophers' claim of the sphere's eternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة لهم : الجهة شيء مشار إليه بالحس - وهو مقصد المتحرك - وكل ما كان كذلك فهو موجود وهذه الجهة محدودة، فلا بد لها من محدد جسماني، ولا يحصل هذا التحديد إلا بالمحيط والمركز، وتقرير هذه المقدمات قد ذكرت في الكتب الحكمية. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : هذا المحدد غير قابل للحركة المستقيمة ؛ لأن الحركة المستقيمة عبارة عن الانتقال من جهة إلى جهة وكل ما صح عليه ذلك كانت الجهة المنتقل عنها والجهة المنتقل إليها محددة قبل ذلك الجسم المنتقل إليها، فيلزم حصول الجهة قبل حصول علتها، وذلك ،محال، وإذا كان كذلك ثبت أن الحركة المستقيمة ممتنعة على الفلك المحدد، وإذا كان كذلك كان الخرق والالتئام ممتنعا عليه.
The third proof for them: direction is something perceptible by sense – and it is the goal of the moving object – and everything like that is existent. This direction is finite, so it must have a corporeal determiner, and this determination does not occur except by the surrounding sphere and the center. The exposition of these premises has been mentioned in the philosophical books. Once this is established, we say: this determiner is not susceptible to rectilinear motion, because rectilinear motion is a transition from one direction to another, and whenever something admits that, the direction from which it moves and the direction to which it moves would be determined before that moving body reaches them, thus the direction would exist before its cause, which is absurd. If that is so, it is established that rectilinear motion is impossible for the determined sphere, and if that is so, then perforation and fusion (kharq wa iltiam) are impossible for it.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is a proof: the philosophers argue that the sphere (falak) is limited and determined by the surrounding sphere and center. Since rectilinear motion would require directions to exist before the moving body reaches them, and those directions are themselves determined by the sphere, this leads to a circular causality. Therefore, the sphere cannot undergo rectilinear motion, and consequently cannot undergo generation and corruption (kharq wa iltiam), implying its eternity.

Key Terms
حركة مستقيمة (rectilinear motion)
علة (cause)
فلك (sphere)
خرق والالتئام (perforation and fusion)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The sphere cannot move at all; it is completely static.
Correction: The argument only denies rectilinear motion; the sphere may move circularly (which philosophers affirm).

Checkpoint
What is the absurdity that arises if the sphere undergoes rectilinear motion?
A) The sphere would break apart
B) The direction would exist before its cause
C) The sphere would leave the universe
D) The motion would be infinite

Key Takeaway
Rectilinear motion would require directions to exist before the sphere that determines them, a circularity that rules out such motion and thus generation/corruption for the sphere.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold that seems logically necessary from your perspective. Are you willing to examine whether that belief actually rests on a logical absurdity, even if it feels comfortable? Commit to identifying one such belief today.
?
The philosophers argued that the sphere cannot move rectilinearly because it would create a contradiction. Is there an area of your life where you are trying to move in a direction that contradicts what you know to be true about yourself? What 'direction' are you striving for that actually depends on something that cannot exist before its cause?
?
Razi's argument shows that some supposed possibilities are actually impossible due to logical necessity. What supposed 'possibilities' in your spiritual life might actually be ruled out by the reality of who Allah is? How does accepting that impossibility lead to deeper submission?

The Philosophers' Fourth Proof: Why Bodies Cannot Cease to Exist
Razi presents the philosophers' argument that bodies cannot be annihilated by examining three possible causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that bodies cannot be annihilated, including their three possible causes of annihilation and why they deem them impossible.

Core Idea
The philosophers argue that annihilation is impossible because neither power nor contraries can produce non-existence.

This passage presents the fourth proof from the philosophers, which attempts to demonstrate the eternity of bodies by arguing that their non-existence is impossible. They systematically consider every conceivable cause of annihilation and reject each one. Understanding this argument is crucial before we see how Sunni theologians respond.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three divisions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the philosophers list three possible causes for annihilation: by an annihilator, by the occurrence of a contrary, or by the removal of a condition.
The passage only elaborates on the first two divisions.
2
Understand why annihilation by an annihilator is rejected
Focus on the argument against the first division: power cannot produce pure non-existence because non-existence is a negation and power is an effective attribute.
This is a key philosophical point about causality and non-being.
3
Understand why annihilation by a contrary is rejected
Read the argument about contraries: since contraries are symmetrical, neither is more likely to annihilate the other. So a contrary cannot cause annihilation.
The text says 'contrariety obtains from both sides'—the existing contrary is as likely to repel the new one as the new one is to annihilate it.
4
Consider the third division (removal of condition)
Note that the passage does not elaborate on this division but declares all three false. Reflect on why removal of condition might also be impossible (e.g., conditions are always present or themselves eternal).
5
Reflect on the overall conclusion
The conclusion is that bodies cannot cease to exist. This is a premise for the philosophers' argument for the eternity of matter. Consider how this fits into the larger theological debate.
This argument is later refuted by Razi from a Sunni perspective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة لهم : عدم الأجسام إما أن يكون بإعدام المعدم أو بطريان الضد أو بزوال الشرط، والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة فالقول بصحة عدم الأجسام ممتنع. وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع حصول الإعدام بالمعدوم وذلك لأن القدرة صفة مؤثرة، والعدم نفي محض، فوقوع العدم بالقدرة يكون محالاً ولما قلنا : إنه يمتنع أن يكون العدم بسبب طريان الضد، وذلك لأن المضادة حاصلة من الجانبين، فليس عدم الضد الباقي لوجود الطارئ أولى من اندفاع الطارئ من لوجود الباقي.
The fourth proof for them: the non-existence of bodies is either by the annihilation of an annihilator, or by the occurrence of a contrary, or by the removal of a condition. All three divisions are false, so the claim that bodies can cease to exist is impossible. We say that it is impossible for annihilation to occur by an annihilator because power (qudra) is an effective attribute, and non-existence is pure negation, so the occurrence of non-existence by power is impossible. And we say that it is impossible for non-existence to be due to the occurrence of a contrary, because contrariety obtains from both sides: the non-existence of the remaining contrary due to the existence of the new one is not more likely than the repulsion of the new one due to the existence of the remaining one.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is a proof that bodies cannot be annihilated. The philosophers divide the possible causes of annihilation into three: an external annihilator (like God), a contrary that overcomes it, or the removal of a condition. They argue all are impossible. Annihilation by an annihilator is impossible because power (qudra) cannot produce pure non-existence. Annihilation by a contrary is impossible because contraries are symmetric: the existing contrary is equally likely to repel the new one as the new one is to annihilate the old. They don't elaborate on the third division (removal of condition) but presumably it is also impossible because conditions are always present or similarly problematic. Thus bodies are eternal.

Key Terms
عدم الأجسام (non-existence of bodies)
إعدام المعدم (annihilation by an annihilator)
طريان الضد (occurrence of a contrary)
زوال الشرط (removal of condition)
قدرة (power)
ضد (contrary)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think this argument denies God's power to annihilate bodies.
Correction: The argument is not about God's power per se, but about the logical impossibility of non-existence being produced by any cause. The philosophers use it to argue for the eternity of matter, which Razi later refutes.

Checkpoint
Why do the philosophers claim that annihilation by an annihilator (like divine power) is impossible?
A) Because God does not have power over non-existence
B) Because power is an effective attribute and non-existence is pure negation, so power cannot produce it
C) Because the annihilator would have to be a body
D) Because annihilation requires a contrary

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that bodies cannot cease to exist because neither an external agent nor a contrary can produce pure non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe God is all-powerful, yet this passage argues that non-existence cannot be caused by power. Does that trouble you? How do you reconcile the idea that God creates existence but not non-existence? Will you sit with this paradox and ask yourself: what does it mean for your understanding of death and annihilation?
?
The philosophers say that two contraries cannot overcome each other because they are symmetric. In your own struggles—say, between faith and doubt—do you assume one will 'win'? How does this argument challenge your view of spiritual growth, and how should it change the way you rely on God rather than on forces within yourself?
?
You often pray for bad habits to 'die' or for trials to 'cease to exist.' But if non-existence is not a thing that can be caused, how should you pray instead? What does this reveal about your need to ask God to create something new (like patience or strength) rather than simply remove something negative?

The Fourth Philosophers' Proof: Why Bodies Cannot Cease to Exist
Razi presents the philosophers' fourth proof that bodies cannot be annihilated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical argument that bodies cannot be annihilated, including the three purported causes and why each is impossible.

Core Idea
Annihilation of bodies fails because every possible cause leads to an absurdity.

Imam Razi presents the fourth argument from philosophers who claim that bodies cannot cease to exist. They argue that annihilation can only occur through one of three causes, yet each is logically impossible. This lesson walks through their reasoning and shows why the conclusion follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation. Notice the threefold division and the refutation of each.
The passage is tightly reasoned; follow each premise.
2
Identify the three possible causes
The passage lists three ways annihilation could happen: by an agent (annihilator), by a contrary, or by removal of a condition. Write them down.
These correspond to the three divisions in the argument.
3
Examine the refutation of each cause
For each cause, see why the philosophers say it is impossible. Understand the reasoning: first, power acts on existence, not non-existence; second, contraries are symmetrical; third, condition removal leads to regress or circularity.
Focus on the logic, not just the conclusion.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The argument concludes that bodies cannot cease to exist. Ask yourself: does this contradict Islamic belief in resurrection? (Hint: the argument is about natural causes, not divine power.)
Razi himself later refutes this argument from an Islamic perspective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة لهم : عدم الأجسام إما أن يكون بإعدام المعدم أو بطريان الضد أو بزوال الشرط، والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة فالقول بصحة عدم الأجسام ممتنع. وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع حصول الإعدام بالمعدوم وذلك لأن القدرة صفة مؤثرة، والعدم نفي محض، فوقوع العدم بالقدرة يكون محالاً ولما قلنا : إنه يمتنع أن يكون العدم بسبب طريان الضد، وذلك لأن المضادة حاصلة من الجانبين، فليس عدم الضد الباقي لوجود الطارئ أولى من اندفاع الطارئ لوجود الباقي. وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع أن يكون لزوال شرط ؛ لأن ذلك الشرط إن كان باقيا كان الكلام في كيفية عدمه، كالكلام في كيفية عدم الأجسام فيفضي إما إلى التسلسل وإما إلى الدور، وإما أن لا يكون باقيًا، وهو أيضًا محال ؛ لأن الباقي يمتنع أن يكون مشروطًا بما لا يكون باقيا. ولما بطلت هذه الأقسام ثبت أن العدم على الأجسام ممتنع.
The fourth proof for them: The annihilation of bodies is either by the act of an annihilator, or by the advent of a contrary, or by the removal of a condition. All three divisions are false, so the claim that bodies can be annihilated is impossible. We say: It is impossible for annihilation to occur by a non-existent, because power is an effective attribute, and non-existence is pure negation, so the occurrence of non-existence by power is impossible. We say: It is impossible for annihilation to occur by the advent of a contrary, because contrariety exists from both sides, so the non-existence of the remaining contrary due to the existence of the arriving one is not more likely than the repulsion of the arriving one due to the existence of the remaining one. We say: It is impossible for it to occur by the removal of a condition, because if that condition remains, then the discussion of how it ceases is like the discussion of how bodies cease, leading either to infinite regress or circularity; and if it does not remain, that is also impossible, because a thing that remains cannot be conditioned by something that does not remain. And when these divisions are invalidated, it is established that annihilation of bodies is impossible.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth philosophical argument against the possibility of bodily annihilation. The philosopher claims that annihilation of bodies can only happen through one of three causes: an external agent causing non-existence, the arrival of a contrary that overcomes the body, or the removal of a condition necessary for its existence. Razi then systematically refutes each: first, an annihilator cannot produce pure non-existence because power affects existence, not non-existence. Second, if a contrary arrives, the two contraries are equally matched, so it is not more likely that the original ceases rather than the newcomer. Third, if a condition is removed, either that condition is itself annihilated (leading to regress) or it remains but the body depends on a non-remaining condition, which is absurd. Thus all possibilities fail, so bodies cannot cease to exist, supporting the philosophers' view of the eternity of the world.

Key Terms
عدم
إعدام
ضد
شرط
تسلسل
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies that God can annihilate bodies on the Day of Resurrection.
Correction: The argument is about natural causes of annihilation, not about divine omnipotence. Razi himself will refute this proof from an Islamic perspective.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth proof, why cannot annihilation occur by the removal of a condition?
A) Because conditions are eternal.
B) Because it leads to infinite regress or circularity.
C) Because removal of a condition is impossible.
D) Because the condition is the body itself.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that since every possible cause of annihilation fails, bodies cannot cease to exist through natural means.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers were absolutely certain of this proof, yet it was later refuted. In what area of your life might you be holding a belief with false certainty? Will you examine it honestly, even if it is uncomfortable?
?
If annihilation by natural means is impossible, does that make you feel invincible? Or does it remind you that your existence is entirely dependent on something beyond natural causes—namely, Allah? How does that dependency shape your daily choices?
?
Consider the 'conditions' you rely on for your spiritual life: prayer, good company, knowledge. If a condition were removed, would your faith falter? What are you doing to ensure your faith is not contingent on fleeting conditions?

Response to the First Objection: Possibility as an Attribute
Razi refutes the first argument by denying the reality of possibility as an attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's refutation of the argument that possibility (imkan) must be an existing attribute requiring an existing subject.

Core Idea
Possibility is not a real attribute, so it cannot support an argument for eternity.

The first philosophical argument claimed that possibility (imkan) is an existing attribute that needs a subject, implying the subject must be eternal. Razi counters by recalling his earlier proof that possibility is not an actual attribute. This short but decisive response targets the core premise of the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read Razi's answer to the first objection
Read the Arabic text and translation. Notice how Razi summarizes the objection in one phrase and then gives his counter.
The key term is 'imkan' (possibility).
2
Understand the objection's logic
The objection argued: possibility is an attribute, attributes need an existing subject, so the subject (body) must exist eternally. Identify where Razi attacks this chain.
3
See Razi's refutation
Razi states that he has already shown that possibility cannot be an existing attribute. This undercuts the entire objection because the first premise is false.
This relies on his earlier discussion on the origination of bodies.
4
Reflect on the method
Notice that Razi does not argue directly about eternity here; he simply refers to an established conclusion. This is a common kalam technique: using prior proofs to counter objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى : أن حاصلها يرجع إلى حرف واحد، وهو الإمكان صفة موجودة ولا بد لها من محل موجود إلا أنا بينا في مسألة حدوث الأجسام أن الإمكان يمتنع أن يكون صفة موجودة. والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية : أن نقول : لا نسلم أنه لو كان الزمان محدثا لكان تقدم عدمه على وجوده بالزمان والدليل عليه أن تقدم الأمس على اليوم ليس بالزمان، وإذا كان كذلك فلم لا يجوز أن يكون تقدم عدم المحدث على وجوده كتقدم بعض أجزاء الزمان على البعض. والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة : أن ما ذكرتم مبني على أن تحدد الجهات لا يمكن إلا بالمحيط والمركز، وفي تقرير هذه المقدمة غموض، وبتقدير أن نسلمها إلا أن هذا يقتضي أن الحركة المستقيمة ممتنعة على هذا المحدد ولا يقتضي أن العدم ممتنع عليه. والجواب عن الشبهة الرابعة أن ما ذكرتم قائم بعينه في الصور والأعراض، أنا نشاهد أنها توجد بعد أن كانت معدومة وتعدم بعد أن كانت موجودة، فكل ما ذكرتموه في هذه الصور والأعراض فهو جوابنا في حق الأجسام.
The answer to the first objection: Its gist reduces to one point: that possibility (imkan) is an existing attribute and must have an existing subject. But we have shown in the issue of the origination of bodies that possibility cannot be an existing attribute. The answer to the second objection: We say: We do not grant that if time were originated, its non-existence would be temporally prior to its existence. The proof is that the priority of yesterday over today is not temporal. If that is the case, why is it not permissible that the priority of the non-existence of the originated thing over its existence be like the priority of some parts of time over others? The answer to the third objection: What you have mentioned is based on the idea that the determination of directions can only occur through the encompassing sphere and the center. There is obscurity in establishing this premise. And even if we grant it, this only entails that rectilinear motion is impossible for this determiner, but it does not entail that annihilation is impossible for it. The answer to the fourth objection: What you have mentioned stands exactly the same in the case of forms and accidents. We observe that they come into existence after being non-existent and cease to exist after being existent. So whatever you have mentioned in the case of these forms and accidents, that is our answer regarding bodies.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage contains Razi's responses to the four philosophical arguments presented earlier. 1. Against the first argument (not fully shown in this window) based on possibility requiring an existing subject, Razi replies that he has already demonstrated in the discussion of the origination of bodies that possibility (imkan) is not a real attribute. 2. Against the second argument about time and priority, he denies that the non-existence of an originated thing is temporally prior to its existence; he gives the example of yesterday and today: yesterday is prior to today but not in time (since time itself is involved). So the priority of non-existence to existence could be like the priority of one part of time over another, not an infinite temporal regress. 3. Against the third argument about direction and the limited body, he questions the premise that directions can only be determined by the encompassing sphere and center, calling it obscure. Even if granted, it only proves the impossibility of rectilinear motion, not the impossibility of annihilation. 4. Against the fourth argument about the impossibility of annihilation, he uses a reductio ad absurdum: the same reasoning would apply to accidents (like colors), which we clearly observe coming into and out of existence. Thus, if the philosophers accept the annihilation of accidents, they cannot deny it for bodies by the same logic.

Key Terms
شبهة
الإمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility (imkan) is a real property that inheres in a subject.
Correction: Razi argues that possibility is not an existing attribute; it is merely a mental consideration.

Checkpoint
What is Razi's main point in his answer to the first objection?
A) Possibility requires an eternal subject.
B) Possibility is an existing attribute.
C) Possibility cannot be an existing attribute.
D) The objection is too complex to answer.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the first argument by denying that possibility (imkan) is a real attribute, thereby invalidating the need for an eternal subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi saw that a seemingly strong objection collapsed because its premise was false. In your life, what 'logical excuse' do you use to justify a sin or neglect that is actually built on a false premise? Will you examine that premise today?
?
The objection assumed possibility is a real attribute without checking. Are there beliefs you hold about yourself or God that you have never questioned because they 'seem obvious'? What would it cost you to honestly re-examine one of them?
?
Razi accepted the authority of a prior proof (huduth al-ajsam). Is there a teaching from revelation or scholarship that you have resisted because you wanted to see all the steps yourself? What would it mean for you to submit to that truth today?

Response to the Second Objection: Time and Priority
Razi denies temporal priority and offers a non-temporal alternative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that the priority of non-existence over existence need not be temporal, using the example of yesterday and today.

Core Idea
Priority can be non-temporal, so no infinite regress follows.

The second objection claimed that if time is originated, then the non-existence of an event must be temporally prior to its existence, leading to an infinite regress. Razi responds by denying that priority is necessarily temporal, illustrating with the relationship between yesterday and today.

Guided Path
1
Read Razi's answer to the second objection
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that Razi begins with 'We do not grant' (la nusallim), a standard kalam rebuttal.
2
Identify the objection's claim
The objection asserted that if time is originated, the non-existence of an originated thing is temporally prior to its existence. Razi denies this.
3
Understand Razi's counter-example
Razi uses the example of yesterday and today. Yesterday is prior to today, but this priority is not temporal because time itself is involved. So priority can be of a different kind.
This shows that non-existence could be prior in a non-temporal sense.
4
See the conclusion
Razi asks: why can't the priority of non-existence over existence be like the priority of one part of time over another? This avoids the infinite regress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى : أن حاصلها يرجع إلى حرف واحد، وهو الإمكان صفة موجودة ولا بد لها من محل موجود إلا أنا بينا في مسألة حدوث الأجسام أن الإمكان يمتنع أن يكون صفة موجودة. والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية : أن نقول : لا نسلم أنه لو كان الزمان محدثا لكان تقدم عدمه على وجوده بالزمان والدليل عليه أن تقدم الأمس على اليوم ليس بالزمان، وإذا كان كذلك فلم لا يجوز أن يكون تقدم عدم المحدث على وجوده كتقدم بعض أجزاء الزمان على البعض. والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة : أن ما ذكرتم مبني على أن تحدد الجهات لا يمكن إلا بالمحيط والمركز، وفي تقرير هذه المقدمة غموض، وبتقدير أن نسلمها إلا أن هذا يقتضي أن الحركة المستقيمة ممتنعة على هذا المحدد ولا يقتضي أن العدم ممتنع عليه. والجواب عن الشبهة الرابعة أن ما ذكرتم قائم بعينه في الصور والأعراض، أنا نشاهد أنها توجد بعد أن كانت معدومة وتعدم بعد أن كانت موجودة، فكل ما ذكرتموه في هذه الصور والأعراض فهو جوابنا في حق الأجسام.
The answer to the first objection: Its gist reduces to one point: that possibility (imkan) is an existing attribute and must have an existing subject. But we have shown in the issue of the origination of bodies that possibility cannot be an existing attribute. The answer to the second objection: We say: We do not grant that if time were originated, its non-existence would be temporally prior to its existence. The proof is that the priority of yesterday over today is not temporal. If that is the case, why is it not permissible that the priority of the non-existence of the originated thing over its existence be like the priority of some parts of time over others? The answer to the third objection: What you have mentioned is based on the idea that the determination of directions can only occur through the encompassing sphere and the center. There is obscurity in establishing this premise. And even if we grant it, this only entails that rectilinear motion is impossible for this determiner, but it does not entail that annihilation is impossible for it. The answer to the fourth objection: What you have mentioned stands exactly the same in the case of forms and accidents. We observe that they come into existence after being non-existent and cease to exist after being existent. So whatever you have mentioned in the case of these forms and accidents, that is our answer regarding bodies.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage contains Razi's responses to the four philosophical arguments presented earlier. 1. Against the first argument (not fully shown in this window) based on possibility requiring an existing subject, Razi replies that he has already demonstrated in the discussion of the origination of bodies that possibility (imkan) is not a real attribute. 2. Against the second argument about time and priority, he denies that the non-existence of an originated thing is temporally prior to its existence; he gives the example of yesterday and today: yesterday is prior to today but not in time (since time itself is involved). So the priority of non-existence to existence could be like the priority of one part of time over another, not an infinite temporal regress. 3. Against the third argument about direction and the limited body, he questions the premise that directions can only be determined by the encompassing sphere and center, calling it obscure. Even if granted, it only proves the impossibility of rectilinear motion, not the impossibility of annihilation. 4. Against the fourth argument about the impossibility of annihilation, he uses a reductio ad absurdum: the same reasoning would apply to accidents (like colors), which we clearly observe coming into and out of existence. Thus, if the philosophers accept the annihilation of accidents, they cannot deny it for bodies by the same logic.

Key Terms
تقدم بالزمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All priority is temporal priority.
Correction: Razi shows that priority can be non-temporal, as with the relationship between parts of time.

Checkpoint
What example does Razi use to show that priority can be non-temporal?
A) The priority of a body over its accidents.
B) The priority of yesterday over today.
C) The priority of the Creator over creation.
D) The priority of a cause over its effect.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the second objection by denying that priority must be temporal, using the relationship between yesterday and today as a counterexample.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that because something didn't happen at the time you expected, it must not be from God? How does Razi's argument about non-temporal priority challenge your assumption that God's timing must match your own?
?
If priority can be non-temporal, then God's command 'Be' is not bound by time. How does this change your view of God's power over your past, present, and future—especially moments you regret or fear?
?
When you think about your own existence before you were born, do you imagine a time when you did not exist? Razi shows that non-existence prior to existence need not be temporal. Does that realization increase your awe of Allah? What would it mean for you to live today knowing that your existence is a direct, non-temporal act of God's will?

Response to the Third Objection: Direction and Motion
Razi questions the premise and limits its consequence to motion, not existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's critique of the argument that determination of directions requires an eternal body, and how he limits its implications.

Core Idea
Direction arguments only rule out rectilinear motion, not annihilation.

The third objection argued that the existence of directions implies a body that determines them, which must be eternal. Razi questions the premise itself, calling it obscure, and even if granted, it only prevents rectilinear motion, not the possibility of annihilation.

Guided Path
1
Read Razi's answer to the third objection
Read the Arabic text. Note that Razi first attacks the premise, then offers a conditional rebuttal.
2
Understand the objection's premise
The objection claimed that determination of directions (e.g., up, down) can only occur through an encompassing sphere and a center, implying an eternal body. Razi says this premise is obscure.
3
See Razi's conditional response
Even if we accept the premise, Razi argues that it only implies that the body cannot move rectilinearly, not that it cannot cease to exist (annihilation).
4
Reflect on the logical distinction
Razi distinguishes between the impossibility of a certain type of motion and the impossibility of annihilation. The objection conflates these.

Source Text

Response to the Fourth Objection: Analogy with Accidents
Razi uses an analogy to accidents to show the argument is self-defeating.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's use of analogy to refute the argument that bodies cannot be annihilated because the same reasoning applies to accidents, which we observe to change.

Core Idea
Whatever applies to bodies must also apply to accidents, but accidents clearly cease, so bodies can too.

The fourth objection argued that bodies, once existent, cannot become non-existent. Razi counters by pointing out that the same reasoning would apply to forms and accidents (like colors), yet we see them come and go. Thus, the objection proves too much, and its logic is flawed.

Guided Path
1
Read Razi's answer to the fourth objection
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice Razi uses a reductio ad absurdum: he applies the objection's logic to accidents.
2
Understand the objection's claim
The objection claimed that bodies cannot transition from existence to non-existence. Razi does not directly argue; instead he says the same reasoning would apply to accidents.
3
See the analogy
Accidents like colors are observed to come into existence and cease. If the philosophers' argument were valid, it would also deny this, but experience contradicts it.
4
Conclude
Therefore, since the philosophers accept the change of accidents, they must also accept the possible annihilation of bodies by the same logic.
