First Proof for Penetration in Celestial Spheres
Razi opens chapter three and presents the first proof for penetration and fusion in celestial spheres.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first proof that celestial spheres can undergo penetration and fusion based on the equality of all bodies in their complete essences.

Core Idea
All bodies share the same essence, so if one can split, all can.

In this chapter, Imam Razi tackles the philosophers' denial that celestial spheres can be penetrated or fused. He presents two proofs; the first is a powerful argument from the metaphysical equality of all bodies. By establishing that all bodies share the same complete essence, Razi shows that what is possible for one body must be possible for all—including the heavens.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: the chapter opening, the statement of the philosophers' denial, the announcement of two proofs, and then the first proof with its premises.
The OCR corrected 'قد تقا تقدم' to 'قد تقدم'.
2
Identify the two premises
The first proof consists of two premises: (1) all bodies are equal in their complete essences, (2) if equal, whatever is valid for one is valid for all. Razi says these were already established in the proof for the Creator's existence.
These premises are metaphysical, not physical.
3
Reflect on the implication
Since we observe penetration and fusion in terrestrial bodies (e.g., water splitting), the premises imply that celestial spheres can also undergo such changes. This challenges the philosophers' view of the heavens as immutable.
Consider why philosophers denied this: they believed celestial matter is different.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث: في بيان أن الخرق والالتئام جائزان على أجرام الفلك. الفلاسفة ينكرون ذلك ولنا : على صحته وجهان. الحجة الأولى : أن جميع الأجسام متساوية في تمام الماهيات، ومتى كان الأمر كذلك فكل ما صح على واحد منها صح على كلها وتقرير هاتين المقدمتين قد تقدم في مسألة إثبات الصانع سبحانه وتعالى.
The third chapter: On demonstrating that penetration and fusion are possible for the celestial spheres. The philosophers deny that. We have two proofs for its validity. The first proof: That all bodies are equal in their complete essences, and when that is the case, then whatever is valid for one of them is valid for all of them. The exposition of these two premises has already been presented in the issue of establishing the Creator, may He be exalted.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage introduces the third chapter, where Razi argues against the philosophers' denial of penetration (kharq) and fusion (ilti'am) in celestial spheres. He states that he has two proofs. The first proof is based on the essential equality of all bodies: all bodies share the same complete essence (mahiyya). Therefore, if a property is possible for one body, it must be possible for all bodies. Since we observe penetration and fusion in sublunar bodies, they must also be possible for celestial spheres. Razi notes that the premises of this argument have already been established in his earlier proof for the existence of God.

Key Terms
خرق
التئام
أجرام الفلك
تمام الماهيات
جائز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof requires accepting that celestial spheres are made of the same material as terrestrial bodies.
Correction: Razi is using a metaphysical principle of essential equality, not material composition. Bodies may differ in physical properties but share the same 'complete essence' (māhiyya) at the level of being a body.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the first proof that penetration is possible in celestial spheres?
A) Celestial spheres are made of fire.
B) All bodies share the same complete essence.
C) The philosophers have no proof against it.
D) It is observed in the heavens.

Key Takeaway
Razi's first proof for penetration in celestial spheres rests on the essential equality of all bodies, meaning that any physical possibility that applies to one body applies to all.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a truth that contradicts your assumptions — like the possibility of change in the heavens — do you cling to your assumptions or submit to the truth? What is holding you back from fully accepting God's power over all things?
?
Think of a time you doubted God's ability to change something in your life. Did your doubt stem from assuming that your limited experience applies to all of creation? How can Razi's argument about essential equality help you replace doubt with certainty?
?
If all bodies share the same essence, then the same divine laws govern the entire universe. How does this consistency strengthen your trust in God's promises? Reflect on a specific promise from the Quran that you find difficult to believe, and ask yourself: is my hesitation rooted in my own limited experience or in a lack of faith in God's absolute power?

The Main Argument Against Non-Existence of Bodies
Razi's proof by division against the possibility of non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's proof that the non-existence of bodies is impossible because any condition for removal leads to infinite regress or circularity, or is itself impossible.

Core Idea
Non-existence of bodies is impossible because it requires a condition that cannot exist.

Razi begins by establishing that if bodies could become non-existent, there would need to be a condition that causes that removal. He then shows that this condition cannot be consistently conceived, thus proving that non-existence is impossible. This argument is foundational for subsequent objections and answers.

Guided Path
1
Read the main argument
Read the Arabic passage from 'وإنما قلنا' to 'ثبت أن العدم على الأجسام ممتنع' along with its translation. Notice how Razi sets up a logical division.
The argument is short but dense; take your time.
2
Identify the logical division
Identify the two cases for a condition of removal: it either remains or does not remain. Understand why each case leads to a contradiction.
This is a classic dichotomy argument.
3
Understand the conclusions
See how the failure of both cases leads to the conclusion that non-existence of bodies is impossible. Reflect on why this is a necessary truth.
Razi's proof is aporetic: it shows that the opposite leads to absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع أن يكون لزوال شرط ؛ لأن ذلك الشرط إن كان باقيا كان الكلام في كيفية عدمه، كالكلام في كيفية عدم الأجسام فيفضي إما إلى التسلسل وإما إلى الدور، وإما أن لا يكون باقيًا، وهو أيضًا محال ؛ لأن الباقي يمتنع أن يكون مشروطًا بما لا يكون باقيا. ولما بطلت هذه الأقسام ثبت أن العدم على الأجسام ممتنع. والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى : أن حاصلها يرجع إلى حرف واحد، وهو الإمكان صفة موجودة ولا بد لها من محل موجود إلا أنا بينا في مسألة حدوث الأجسام أن الإمكان يمتنع أن يكون صفة موجودة. والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية : أن نقول : لا نسلم أنه لو كان الزمان محدثًا لكان تقدم عدمه على وجوده بالزمان والدليل عليه أن تقدم الأمس على اليوم ليس بالزمان، وإذا كان كذلك فلم لا يجوز أن يكون تقدم عدم المحدث على وجوده كتقدم بعض أجزاء الزمان على البعض. والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة : أن ما ذكرتم مبني على أن تحدد الجهات لا يمكن إلا بالمحيط والمركز، وفي تقرير هذه المقدمة غموض، وبتقدير أن نسلمها إلا أن هذا يقتضي أن الحركة المستقيمة ممتنعة على هذا المحدد ولا يقتضي أن العدم ممتنع عليه. والجواب عن الشبهة الرابعة أن ما ذكرتم قائم بعينه في الصور والأعراض، أنا نشاهد أنها توجد بعد أن كانت معدومة وتعدم بعد أن كانت موجودة، فكل ما ذكرتموه في هذه الصور والأعراض فهو جوابنا في حق الأجسام.
We only say: It is impossible for [something] to have a condition for its removal because that condition, if it remains, then the discussion about how it ceases would be like the discussion about how bodies cease, leading either to infinite regress or circularity, or it is not remaining, and that is also impossible because a permanent thing cannot be conditioned by that which is not permanent. And since these divisions are invalid, it is established that nonexistence of bodies is impossible. The answer to the first objection: Its essence reduces to one point, namely that possibility is an existing attribute and must have an existing subject, except we have shown in the problem of the origination of bodies that possibility cannot be an existing attribute. The answer to the second objection: We say: We do not grant that if time were originated, its prior nonexistence would be temporal priority. The evidence for this is that the priority of yesterday over today is not temporal. If that is so, why is it not permissible that the priority of the nonexistence of an originated thing over its existence be like the priority of some parts of time over others? The answer to the third objection: What you mentioned is based on the fact that delimitation of directions can only be by the encompassing and the center, and there is obscurity in establishing this premise; and granting we accept it, it only requires that rectilinear motion is impossible for this delimited body, but it does not require that non-existence is impossible for it. The answer to the fourth objection: What you mentioned applies equally to forms and accidents. We observe that they come into existence after being nonexistent and cease to exist after being existent. So everything you mentioned in these forms and accidents is our answer regarding bodies.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage refutes a claim that bodies can become non-existent. The author first argues that positing a condition for removal leads to absurdities, so non-existence is impossible. Then he answers four objections. The first objection claims possibility is an existing attribute requiring a subject; the author replies that possibility is not an existing attribute as shown earlier. The second objection concerns temporal priority; the author argues priority is not necessarily temporal, using the analogy of yesterday and today. The third objection involves delimitation of directions; the author questions its premise and notes it only affects rectilinear motion, not non-existence. The fourth objection uses examples of forms and accidents; the author says this pattern is the same as bodies, so it does not refute their case. The overall argument is that based on the condition analysis, non-existence of bodies is rationally impossible.

عدم
شرط
تسلسل
دور

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what happens if the condition for removal of a body remains?
A) It leads to infinite regress or circularity
B) The body becomes permanent
C) The condition itself becomes non-existent
D) It proves the body is originated

Key Takeaway
Razi proves the impossibility of bodies becoming non-existent by showing that any condition for removal leads to absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi proves that bodies cannot cease to exist because any condition leads to absurdity. Do you live as though the material world around you is permanent? What would change if you truly internalized that only God is eternal? This week, consciously detach from one material object and thank God for His permanence.
?
The argument rejects infinite regress as a logical impossibility. In your life, do you ever find yourself trusting in a chain of causes without ultimately resting on God? Examine one area where you rely on worldly means without acknowledging the First Cause. Make a deliberate act of tawakkul today.
?
Razi's reasoning shows that the non-existence of bodies is contradictory. Yet many people live in fear of losing everything. How does this argument challenge your fear of loss? Commit to reciting 'Hasbunallah wa ni'mal wakeel' when fear arises, trusting that what God has decreed will not cease.

Refuting Objections from Possibility and Temporal Priority
Razi's responses to the first two objections about possibility and time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's answers to the first two objections: that possibility is not an existing attribute, and that temporal priority is not the only kind of priority.

Core Idea
Possibility is not an attribute, and priority need not be temporal.

After establishing the main argument, Razi addresses four objections. The first objection claims that possibility is an existing attribute requiring a subject. The second argues that if time is originated, its non-existence is temporally prior. Razi's responses are concise and rely on earlier doctrines.

Guided Path
1
Read both objections and answers
Read the Arabic for the first two objections and their answers ('والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى' and 'والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية'). Follow the translation carefully.
Each objection is short; note how Razi reuses arguments from earlier topics.
2
Understand the first objection
The objection says possibility is an attribute existing in a subject; without a subject, possibility would be impossible. Razi replies that possibility is not an existing attribute, as shown in the problem of origination.
This ties back to a previous discussion on the nature of possibility.
3
Understand the second objection
The objection states that if time is originated, its prior non-existence must be temporal. Razi denies that priority is necessarily temporal, using the example of yesterday and today.
Razi distinguishes between temporal priority and logical priority.

Source Text

Refuting Objections from Delimitation and Accidents
Razi's responses to the third and fourth objections about delimitation and accidental change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's responses to the third and fourth objections: that the argument from delimitation only affects motion, not existence, and that the pattern of forms and accidents does not undermine his position.

Core Idea
Delimitation arguments only restrict motion, and the pattern of accidents is analogous to bodies.

Razi now addresses the last two objections. The third objection involves the need for a center and encompassing sphere to delimit directions, implying that bodies cannot be non-existent. The fourth points to the observed coming-to-be and passing-away of forms and accidents. Razi's answers are sharp and bring the discussion to a close.

Guided Path
1
Read the last two objections and answers
Read the Arabic for the third and fourth objections and their answers. Notice how each response challenges the premises or shows consistency.
These answers are more technical; read slowly.
2
Analyze the third objection
The objection claims that delimitation of directions requires an encompassing sphere and center, with implications for non-existence. Razi questions the premise and notes that even if accepted, it only prevents rectilinear motion from the boundary body, not non-existence.
This objection is about celestial bodies and their limits.
3
Analyze the fourth objection
The objection says forms and accidents constantly come and go, so why not bodies? Razi replies that this pattern applies equally to bodies, so it is not a problem—it actually supports his view.
Razi turns the objection back on itself.

Source Text

Second Proof: Simplicity and Symmetry of Spheres
Razi's second proof for the possibility of perforation using the simplicity of spheres.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second proof for the possibility of perforation in celestial spheres based on the simplicity and symmetry of their sides.

Core Idea
If a sphere is simple, its opposite sides are identical in essence — so what is possible for one is possible for the other.

Razi continues his arguments for the possibility of perforation in celestial spheres. In this second proof, he uses a logical argument from the nature of simple bodies. If a sphere is simple, its two sides are equal in quiddity, which means they share all possibilities. This leads to the conclusion that perforation and rejoining are rationally possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Note how Razi structures his argument: a conditional statement followed by an example.
The passage is concise but contains a full logical proof.
2
Identify the key logical move
From the equality of two sides in quiddity, Razi infers that whatever is possible for one side is possible for the other. This is the core of the proof.
Why does equality of sides lead to transfer of possibilities? Because sides are identical in essence.
3
Reflect on the example
Razi gives the example of the moon: its concave side could touch fire and its convex side Mercury's sphere, and the opposite is also possible. This shows that perforation is possible because sides are interchangeable.
The example illustrates the abstract principle with a concrete celestial sphere.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : أن كل واحد من هذه الأفلاك إما أن يكون بسيطا أو مركباً من البسائط، وكل ما كان بسيطا فإن كل واحد من جانبيه متساويان في تمام الماهية إذ لو لم يكن كذلك لكان البسيط مركبا هذا خلف، وإذا كان كل واحد من جانبيه متساويين في تمام الماهية للجانب الآخر فكل ما صح على أحد الجانبين صح على الآخر، فكما أن ذلك القمر يصح أن يماس بمقعره النار وبمحدبه كرة عطارد و أن يكون عكسه ممكنا ومتى كان الخرق والالتئام جائزين على الأفلاك.
The second proof: Each of these spheres is either simple or composed of simples. Whatever is simple, each of its two sides is equal in complete quiddity, because if it were otherwise, the simple would be compound, which is a contradiction. And when each of its two sides is equal in complete quiddity to the other side, then whatever is possible for one side is possible for the other. Thus, just as it is possible for that moon to touch with its concave side the fire and with its convex side the sphere of Mercury, and the opposite is possible, so perforation and rejoining are possible on the spheres.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage provides the second proof. The author classifies spheres as either simple (بسيط) or compound (مركب). For a simple sphere, the two opposite sides are identical in quiddity (تمام الماهية). If they were not equal, the simple would be compound, which is a contradiction. Therefore, whatever is possible for one side is possible for the other. As an example, the moon's concave side could theoretically touch fire and its convex side touch Mercury's sphere, and the opposite arrangement is also possible. Hence, perforation and rejoining are possible because the sides are interchangeable.

Key Terms
بسيط
مركب
جانب
مقعر
محدب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof claims that perforation actually occurs in celestial spheres.
Correction: The proof only establishes rational possibility, not actual occurrence. Razi is arguing that perforation is not impossible, not that it happens in reality.

Checkpoint
What would happen if the two sides of a simple sphere were not equal in quiddity?
A) The sphere would be compound
B) The sphere would be destroyed
C) The sphere would become an accident
D) The sphere would have no motion

Key Takeaway
The simplicity of celestial spheres implies that their opposite sides are identical, making perforation rationally possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You trust that God's creation is perfect and unchanging in its order. But Razi argues that even celestial spheres, the most perfect of bodies, are not beyond change. Does this unsettle your confidence in God's constancy, or does it expand your understanding of His power? What would it mean for your daily reliance on God if you truly believed that nothing in the universe—not even the heavens—is fixed beyond His will?
?
Think of a time you believed something was impossible because it contradicted the 'laws' you assumed were absolute. How does Razi's insistence on rational possibility challenge you to re-examine your own assumptions about what God can and cannot do? Will you now be quicker to say 'God is able' even when your mind says 'that's impossible'?
?
Razi's proof relies on the idea that if two things are identical in essence, what is possible for one is possible for the other. Consider your own spiritual state: Do you treat your own capacity for change as limited, forgetting that God can transform you as easily as He could perforate a sphere? What is one area of your life where you've assumed change is impossible, and how will you actively surrender that assumption to God today?

Razi's Refutation: Confining the Objection to the Outermost Sphere
Razi confines the philosophers' objection to the outermost sphere.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that the philosophers' objection to perforation applies only to the outermost sphere, not to all celestial spheres.

Core Idea
The philosophers' argument only hits the outermost sphere, not all spheres.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi responds to a key philosophical objection against the possibility of perforation and rejoining (al-kharq wa al-ilti'am) in the celestial spheres. The philosophers claimed that such processes require rectilinear motion, which is impossible for spheres. Razi's clever response restricts the scope of their argument, showing it does not affect the inner spheres. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection and response
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note how Razi presents the philosophers' argument and his answer.
2
Understand the key concepts
Focus on the terms 'rectilinear motion' (حركة مستقيمة) and 'the outermost sphere' (الفلك الأقصى). Why does Razi distinguish this sphere?
3
Consider the implications
Ask: why is it important that the argument only applies to the outermost sphere? What does this show about Razi's method of refutation?
Notice that Razi does not necessarily accept the premise; he shows the objection is not general.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الفلاسفة فقد احتجوا على امتناع ذلك بأن قالوا: الخرق والالتئام لا يحصلان إلا بالحركة المستقيمة، لكن الحركة المستقيمة على أجرام الفلك ممتنعة، فوجب القطع بامتناع الخرق والالتئام عليها. والجواب : أن هذا الكلام - وإن صح - لكن لا يتمشى إلا في الفلك الذي هو الجسم المحدد للجهات، وهو الفلك الأقصى، وأما سائر الأفلاك فلا يجري فيها البتة.
As for the philosophers, they argued for the impossibility of that by saying: Perforation and rejoining do not occur except by rectilinear motion, but rectilinear motion is impossible for the celestial spheres, so it is necessary to conclude the impossibility of perforation and rejoining upon them. The answer: This statement - even if sound - only applies to the sphere which is the body that delimits the directions, which is the outermost sphere. As for the other spheres, it does not apply at all.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' objection and Razi's answer. The philosophers argue that perforation requires rectilinear motion (حركة مستقيمة), but celestial spheres only move circularly and cannot have rectilinear motion, so perforation is impossible. Razi's response is that this argument, even if accepted, only applies to the outermost sphere (الفلك الأقصى), which delimits the directions (محدد الجهات). For all other spheres, rectilinear motion is not necessarily impossible because they are not the delimiting body. Therefore, the objection is not general and does not prove the impossibility for all spheres.

Key Terms
حركة مستقيمة
محدد الجهات
الفلك الأقصى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Razi concedes that perforation requires rectilinear motion and then only limits the impossibility to the outermost sphere.
Correction: Razi does not concede the premise; he says 'even if sound' (wa in sahha), indicating he does not necessarily accept it. He merely shows that even if accepted, the argument does not generalize.

Checkpoint
Which sphere does Razi say the philosophers' argument applies to?
A) The sphere of the moon
B) The sphere of Mercury
C) The outermost sphere
D) All spheres equally

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the philosophers by showing their argument only applies to the outermost sphere, leaving other spheres unaffected.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often assume your understanding of a truth applies universally, like the philosophers assuming their argument covered all spheres. Where in your life have you mistaken a partial insight for a complete one? Will you pause today to ask what 'outermost sphere' might be limiting your view?
?
Razi accepted the philosophers' premise for the sake of argument to expose its limit. When you disagree with someone, do you first try to see where their reasoning might be valid before rejecting it? What would it cost you to adopt that approach with someone you oppose?
?
The outermost sphere delimits all directions—it defines the frame of reference. What assumption about Allah, creation, or yourself has become your 'outermost sphere'—so familiar you never question it? Are you willing to step beyond it tonight in prayer, asking Allah to expand your vision?

First Proof That Souls Are Not Eternal: Unity and Multiplicity
Razi argues that if souls were eternal, they would be either one or many, both impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that souls cannot be eternal because they cannot be either one or many in pre-eternity.

Core Idea
Souls cannot be eternal because neither unity nor multiplicity is possible without a body.

In this passage, Imam Razi presents the first of two proofs that souls are not eternal. He uses a disjunctive syllogism: if souls were eternal, they would be either one or many. Both possibilities are shown to be impossible. The argument relies on the need for a distinguisher for multiplicity and the absurdity of shared identity if souls were one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the logical structure: 'either...or' and 'both are false'.
2
Identify the two branches
The proof splits into two possibilities: souls are one or many. Mark where the author discusses each.
The first branch ends with 'هذا خلف', the second with 'امتنع اختلافها'.
3
Analyze the proof against unity
If souls were one, after attachment to bodies they would remain one, leading to absurd consequences (my soul = your soul, shared knowledge).
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Analyze the proof against multiplicity
If souls were many in pre-eternity, they need a distinguisher. Essential distinguisher fails (same species); accidental distinguisher fails (no substrate before bodies).
The distinguisher (مميز) is key.
5
Conclude the proof
Both unity and multiplicity are impossible, so souls cannot be eternal. This supports their createdness (حدوث).
The conclusion is re-stated at the end.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما القول الثاني - وهو أنها تفنى عند موت البدن - فقد احتجوا بأن هذه النفوس ليست أزلية، فوجب أن لا تكون أبدية. وإنما قلنا : إنها ليست أزلية لوجهين : الوجه الأول : أن النفوس لو كانت أزلية لكانت إما أن تكون واحدة أو كثيرة، والقسمان باطلان فبطل القول بكونها أزلية. إنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع كونها واحدة لأنها بعد التعلق بالأبدان إن بقيت واحدة كان نفسي نفسك، وبالعكس، فكل ما كان معلوما لإنسان يجب أن يكون معلوما لكل إنسان. هذا خلف . وإن تعددت فهو محال ؛ لأن هاتين النفسين اللتين هما موجودتان الآن إن قلنا : إنهما كانتا موجودتين قبل التعلق بالأبدان، فقبل التعلق بالأبدان كانت النفوس كثيرة لا واحدة وإن قلنا بأنهما ما كانتا موجودتين، فهما قد حدثنا الآن فلا تكون هذه النفوس قديمة . وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع كونها متعددة في الأزل ؛ لأن ذلك التعدد لا بد فيه من مميز، وذلك المميز إما أن يكون ذاتيّا أو عرضياً، لا جائز أن يكون ذاتيا ؛ لأن النفوس البشرية متحدة بالنوع ولا جائز أن يكون عرضيّاً ؛ لأن الامتياز بالصفات العرضية تابع لاختلاف المواد ومادة النفس هي البدن فقبل التعلق بالأبدان ليس لها شيء من المواد فامتنع اختلافها بسبب الصفات العرضية، فثبت أن النفوس البشرية لو كانت أزلية لكانت في الأزل إما واحدة أو متعددة، والقسمان باطلان، فبطل القول بكونها أزلية.
As for the second view — that they perish at the death of the body — they argued that these souls are not eternal, so it must be that they are not everlasting. And we say: They are not eternal for two reasons. The first reason: If souls were eternal, they would be either one or many, and both divisions are false, so the claim of their being eternal is false. We say: It is impossible for them to be one, because after attachment to bodies, if they remained one, then my soul would be your soul, and vice versa, so everything known to one person would have to be known to every person — that is absurd. And if they were multiple, that is impossible because these two souls that exist now: if we say they existed before attachment to bodies, then before attachment to bodies souls were many, not one; and if we say they did not exist, then they come into existence now, so these souls are not eternal. And we say: It is impossible for them to be multiple in pre-eternity, because that multiplicity necessarily requires a distinguisher, and that distinguisher is either essential or accidental. It cannot be essential because human souls are one in species. It cannot be accidental because distinction by accidental attributes follows from difference of substrates, and the substrate of the soul is the body; so before attachment to bodies there is for them no substrate, so their difference due to accidental attributes is impossible. Thus it is established that if human souls were eternal, they would be in pre-eternity either one or many, and both divisions are false, so the claim of their being eternal is false.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage is a proof that souls are not eternal, presented as the first of two reasons. The author is responding to the second view (that souls perish at death), whose proponents argued that souls are not eternal, so they cannot be everlasting. The author aims to prove their non-eternality (حدوث). The first reason uses a disjunctive syllogism: if souls were eternal, they would be either one or many. Both options are impossible. First, if they were one in pre-eternity, then after attachment to bodies, if they remained one, all souls would share the same identity, leading to the consequence that your soul and mine would be the same, and thus every person would know everything known by every other — which is false. Second, if they were many in pre-eternity, that requires a distinguisher (مميز) to differentiate them. The distinguisher cannot be essential because all human souls share the same essence; it cannot be accidental because accidental distinctions depend on the substrate (المادة), which for the soul is the body. Before attachment to bodies, there are no substrates, so accidental distinctions are impossible. Therefore, souls cannot be many in pre-eternity. Since both unity and multiplicity are impossible, souls cannot be eternal. This establishes that they are created (non-eternal).

Key Terms
أزلية
أبدية
الوحدة
الكثرة
المميز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that this argument proves souls are created ex nihilo at the moment of death, but it only proves they are not eternal; they could have been created at some time before death.
Correction: The argument only establishes that souls are not pre-eternal (i.e., they have a beginning). It does not specify when they begin.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why is it impossible for souls to be many in pre-eternity?
A) Because multiplicity requires a distinguisher, and there is no possible distinguisher before bodies exist.
B) Because souls are not material.
C) Because multiplicity would imply they are bodies.
D) Because they are all one.

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that souls cannot be eternal because neither unity nor multiplicity is logically possible for them apart from bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Are you treating your body as a mere vessel for a pre-existing soul, or as the very substrate through which your soul has been given distinction and identity? What will you change today in how you care for your body as a gift from your Creator?
?
Razi argues that if all souls were one, every person would know what every other knows. Reflect on the privacy of your own thoughts. Do you appreciate that your unique, private consciousness is a sign of the soul's createdness and dependence on a body? How does this make you more grateful to Allah for the distinct personhood He has given you?
?
The argument shows that distinction between souls only arises through accidental attributes that depend on the body. Since your body is the substrate of your soul's identity, are you aware that your physical actions and habits shape your soul's attributes? What is one bodily habit you need to change to align your soul with righteousness?

Two Proofs That Souls Are Not Everlasting
Al-Razi gives two arguments that souls cannot be everlasting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's two arguments for why souls cannot be everlasting and the logical basis for each.

Core Idea
Souls cannot be everlasting because infinite actual numbers are impossible and because they are inherently receptive to non-existence.

After establishing that souls are not pre-eternal, Al-Razi now turns to refute the view that they are everlasting. He offers two distinct proofs: one based on the impossibility of an actual infinite number of souls, and another based on the necessary receptivity to non-existence that comes from their origination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: a general claim followed by 'two proofs' (وجهان).
2
Understand the first proof
Trace the logic: celestial cycles have no beginning → bodies originate without beginning → souls originate without beginning. If souls were permanent, they would now be infinite in number, which is impossible because any actual number is finite (can increase/decrease).
This proof assumes the kalām position that celestial cycles have no beginning, not that the world is pre-eternal.
3
Understand the second proof
Focus on the concept of quiddity (ماهية). Souls came from non-existence, so their quiddity has the property of being receptive to non-existence. This property is a necessary concomitant (لازم), so they are always susceptible to non-existence. Therefore, one cannot be certain they will never perish.
The second proof does not prove that souls will perish, only that we cannot be certain of their everlastingness.
4
Compare and reflect
Note that both proofs conclude that souls are not necessarily everlasting (أبدية), but they use different grounds: one from external (infinite accumulation) and one from internal (receptivity to non-existence).
Al-Razi leaves open the possibility that God may preserve them, but their essence does not guarantee permanence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : وجب أن لا تكون أبدية، ويدل عليه وجهان: الأول : أن سبب حدوث النفس عن العلل الفلكية العالية ليس إلا حدوث الأبدان المستعدة لقبولها لمن دورات الفلك لا أول لها فحدوث الأبدان لا أول له، فحدوث النفوس لا أول له فلو كانت النفوس باقية لزم أن يكون قد حصل الآن نفوس غير متناهية وذلك محال ؛ لأن كل عدد موجود فإنه قابل للزيادة والنقصان، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو متناه.
الثاني : : أن هذه النفوس لما وجدت بعد أن كانت معدومة كانت ماهياتها قابلة العدم، وهذه القابلية من لوازمها فوجب أن تكون قابلة للعدم أبداً، وإذا كان كذلك امتنع القطع بأنها لا تعدم البتة.
If this is established, we say: it is necessary that they are not everlasting (abadiyya). Two proofs indicate this. The first: that the cause of the origination of the soul from the high celestial causes is nothing but the origination of bodies prepared to receive it, because the cycles of the celestial sphere have no beginning, so the origination of bodies has no beginning, so the origination of souls has no beginning. So if souls were remaining (baqiya), it would entail that now an infinite number of souls have come to exist, and that is impossible; because every existing number is susceptible to increase and decrease, and everything that is such is finite. The second: that these souls, since they came to exist after having been non-existent, their quiddities (mahiyyat) were receptive to non-existence, and this receptivity is among their necessary concomitants, so they must be receptive to non-existence forever. And if that is so, it is impossible to be certain that they will never cease to exist at all.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage continues the argument after establishing that souls are not pre-eternal. The author now aims to prove that they are not everlasting (abadi). Two proofs are given. The first proof is based on the celestial cycles: since the heavens cycle without beginning, bodies (and hence souls) are originated without beginning. If souls were permanent (baqiya), an infinite number of souls would accumulate now—an actual infinite, which is impossible because any existing number is finite (since it can be increased or decreased). The second proof is based on the intrinsic contingency of souls: they came into existence after non-existence, so their quiddities have the property of being receptive to non-existence. This receptivity is a necessary concomitant, so they are always susceptible to non-existence; hence one cannot be certain they will never perish. Note that the author does not conclude they will perish, only that their permanence is not necessary.

Key Terms
أبدية
علل فلكية
دورات الفلك
غير متناهية
ماهية
قابلية العدم
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first proof assumes the world is pre-eternal and thus contradicts Islamic teachings.
Correction: The proof does not assume pre-eternity; it assumes that celestial cycles have no beginning, which is a kalām position that does not entail the world's pre-eternity. The cycles are originated but without a first moment in a sense.

Checkpoint
Why does the first proof conclude that an infinite number of souls cannot exist now?
A) Because souls are immaterial and thus not subject to counting.
B) Because every existing number is finite, as it can be increased or decreased.
C) Because souls are created at the same time as their bodies.
D) Because celestial cycles have a beginning.

Key Takeaway
Souls are not necessarily everlasting because an infinite accumulation is impossible and their contingent nature prevents certainty of permanent existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The second proof says we cannot be certain our souls will never cease. Does that uncertainty frighten you, or does it humble you? How should it change your attachment to this world and its transient pleasures?
?
The impossibility of an actual infinite challenges your mind to accept limits. Are you willing to submit to God's revelation when your intellect cannot fully comprehend the nature of souls? What does your response say about your trust in divine wisdom?

First Objection: Miracles Are Impossible
Razi presents the first objection against miracles: they are impossible and lead to scepticism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against the use of miracles as evidence for prophethood, namely that miracles are impossible and would lead to scepticism.

Core Idea
If miracles are possible, nothing can be certain — scepticism follows.

This lesson presents a powerful objection to the argument from miracles. The objector denies that it is even possible for natural habits to be broken (kharq al-‘ādāt). He argues that admitting such a possibility would open the door to total uncertainty, where anything could happen at any moment. This would undermine all knowledge and lead to scepticism (safsatah).

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the original Arabic once, then the literal translation. Notice the logical flow: the objection is stated, then the denial, then the proof by leading to scepticism.
The passage is cut off mid-sentence; the answer will come later.
2
Identify the objector's main claim
The objector denies the possibility of kharq al-‘ādāt (breaking of habits). He claims that without this possibility, the miracle argument is invalid.
3
Understand the consequence
The objector argues that if miracles were possible, natural laws could be broken at any time, leading to arbitrary changes. This would destroy all certainty and result in scepticism.
Consider the examples: mountains turning to gold, sea to blood.
4
Reflect on the incomplete argument
The passage ends abruptly. The author's response to this objection is not included here. Keep this in mind as you study the subsequent lessons.

If it is said: The objection to this indication is from several aspects: The first objection: that the statement 'a miracle (kharq al-‘ādah) appeared at the hand of the claimant of prophethood' is only valid if breaking of habits is possible, and we deny this possibility. The proof that it is impossible is that allowing it leads to sophistry (safsatah): if we open this door, we cannot be safe from mountains turning into pure gold, from sea water turning into pure blood, from this person we now observe being an old man becoming decrepit, and from this happening in...
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage introduces the first objection against the argument from miracles. The objector denies the possibility of miracles (kharq al-‘ādāt). He argues that if we allow that natural laws can be broken, then we cannot be certain of anything; the world could change arbitrarily. This would lead to scepticism (safsatah). He gives examples: mountains turning to gold, sea to blood, and an old man becoming decrepit. The objection is that miracles cannot be used as evidence if they are not possible. The passage is cut off, so the answer is not included in this window.

Two Paths of Reasoning: Philosopher vs Theologian on Causality
Razi contrasts the philosopher's and theologian's methods regarding divine action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fundamental methodological difference between the philosopher and the theologian regarding causality and divine action.

Core Idea
One argues from cause to effect; the other from effect to cause.

In classical Islamic thought, philosophers and theologians often reached opposite conclusions about the world's eternity. This passage from Imam Razi pinpoints the exact reason: they start their reasoning from different ends. The philosopher begins with God's eternal nature and deduces that His effect must also be eternal. The theologian begins with the observable fact that acts are preceded by non-existence and concludes that God created the world. By understanding this contrast, you’ll see how starting premises shape entire theological systems.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Read the Arabic once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the parallel structure: 'al-faylasufu yaqul... wa al-mutakallimu yaqul...'
This parallelism highlights the contrast.
2
Identify the two arguments
Extract the philosopher's argument (from God's perpetuity to the effect's perpetuity) and the theologian's argument (from the effect's non-existence to God's creative act).
The philosopher deduces from cause to effect; the theologian from effect to cause.
3
Reflect on the core disagreement
Razi identifies 'the premise of necessity and contingency in essence and attributes' as the real battleground. Contemplate why this premise is decisive for each school.
This is the 'great battle and great calamity' he mentions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن الفلسفي يقول : ما لأجله كان مؤثرًا في غيره إما أن يكون هو ذاته أو لوازم ذاته فيلزم من دوام ذاته دوام مؤثريته ودوام أثره. والمتكلم يقول: لما وجب في الفعل أن يكون مسبوقا بالعدم لزم أن يقال: إنه أوجد بعد أن لم يكن موجودا فالفيلسوف يستدل بحال المؤثر على حال الأثر، والمتكلم يستدل بحال الأثر على المؤثر، والمعركة الكبرى والطامة العظمى في هذا الموضوع هي مقدمة الوجوب والإمكان في الذات والصفات، وبالله التوفيق.
Then the philosopher says: That by which He is an effecter on others is either His essence or the concomitants of His essence, so from the perpetuity of His essence follows the perpetuity of His effectivity and the perpetuity of His effect. And the theologian says: Since it is necessary for the act to be preceded by non-existence, it must be said that He brought it into existence after it was not existent. So the philosopher infers from the state of the cause to the state of the effect, and the theologian infers from the state of the effect to the state of the cause. And the great battle and great calamity in this subject is the premise of necessity and contingency in essence and attributes. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage contrasts two methods: The philosopher (faylasūf) argues that God's effectivity is eternal because He is eternal, so His effect (the world) must also be eternal. The theologian (mutakallim) argues from the fact that acts are preceded by non-existence (i.e., the world is created), so God must have brought it into existence after it did not exist. Thus, the philosopher deduces from the cause to the effect, while the theologian deduces from the effect to the cause. Razi identifies the core issue as the premise of necessity (wujūb) and contingency (imkān) in essence and attributes. This is the major point of contention between the two schools regarding the eternity of the world.

Key Terms
فيلسوف (philosopher)
متكلم (theologian)
مؤثر (effecter)
أثر (effect)
دوام (perpetuity)
عدم (non-existence)
وجوب (necessity)
إمكان (contingency)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume Razi is refuting the philosopher in this passage.
Correction: Razi is not refuting; he is objectively contrasting two methodologies to clarify the root of the disagreement.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the fundamental disagreement between the philosopher and the theologian?
A) Whether God is one or many.
B) Whether one argues from cause to effect or effect to cause.
C) Whether God has attributes.
D) Whether prophecy is possible.

Key Takeaway
The philosopher and theologian differ in their starting point: one begins with God's nature, the other with the world's createdness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about why you believe in God, do you naturally start from the world around you (like the theologian) or from what you know about God (like the philosopher)? Which approach feels more certain to you—and why does that matter for your faith?
?
The philosopher's argument would make the world eternal and God's act necessary. Does the idea of a God who creates out of necessity feel less personal to you than a God who creates by free will? How does that affect how you relate to Him in worship?
?
If you had to explain your belief in God to someone who doubts, would you use the theologian's method (starting from creation) or the philosopher's (starting from God's nature)? What does your choice reveal about whether you trust the world to point to God, or God's perfection to point to creation?
